    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

        state: 'apple_oauth_state_bf49bbc0-66df-49e7-ba67-2a13a27e24ed'

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11643053828742087638">When Colton Burpo made it through an emergency appendectomy, his family was overjoyed at his miraculous survival. What they weren't expecting, though, was the story that emerged in the months that followed--a story as beautiful as it was extraordinary, detailing their little boy's trip to heaven and back.<br/><br/>Colton, not yet four years old, told his parents he left his body dur</span>
  <span id="freeText11643053828742087638" style="display:none">When Colton Burpo made it through an emergency appendectomy, his family was overjoyed at his miraculous survival. What they weren't expecting, though, was the story that emerged in the months that followed--a story as beautiful as it was extraordinary, detailing their little boy's trip to heaven and back.<br /><br />Colton, not yet four years old, told his parents he left his body during the surgery--and authenticated that claim by describing exactly what his parents were doing in another part of the hospital while he was being operated on. He talked of visiting heaven and relayed stories told to him by people he met there whom he had never met in life, sharing events that happened even before he was born. He also astonished his parents with descriptions and obscure details about heaven that matched the Bible exactly, though he had not yet learned to read.<br /><br />With disarming innocence and the plainspoken boldness of a child, Colton tells of meeting long-departed family members. He describes Jesus, the angels, how "really, really big" God is, and how much God loves us. Retold by his father, but using Colton's uniquely simple words, "Heaven is for Real" offers a glimpse of the world that awaits us, where as Colton says, "Nobody is old and nobody wears glasses."<br /><br />"Heaven is for Real" will forever change the way you think of eternity, offering you the chance to see, and believe, like a child.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11643053828742087638" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

While reading this book I felt like the kid was simply a pawn being used for his father's own agenda. <br />And in response to Cathy's previous answer that the kid had recanted - no, he didn't. She's thinking of as different kid from a different book who said he went to heaven but recently recanted. Colton Burpo still maintains its all true, which i'm sure his brainwashed head really believes<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/142886088?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*" href="/user/show/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius">Stephanie *Extremely Stable Genius*</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1085121-stephanie-extremely-stable-genius?shelf=pure-crap">pure-crap</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer142886088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16510061730134759276">Oh Boy.<br/><br/>I am reluctant to review this since I have friends who loved this book, and my mother recommended it. But this book bugged me so much I just have to have my say.<br/><br/>This is a book about a three year old boy, Colton, who comes close to death (never pronounced clinically dead, as in most <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near_death_experience' rel='nofollow noopener'>near death experiences</a> ), Claims that he hung out with Jesus, God, John the Baptist, Gabriel, the Holy Ghost (I've always wondered about the holy ghost), his great grandpa, his dead sister, and Jesus's rainbow </span>
  <span id="freeText16510061730134759276" style="display:none">Oh Boy.<br /><br />I am reluctant to review this since I have friends who loved this book, and my mother recommended it. But this book bugged me so much I just have to have my say.<br /><br />This is a book about a three year old boy, Colton, who comes close to death (never pronounced clinically dead, as in most <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near_death_experience" rel="nofollow noopener">near death experiences</a> ), Claims that he hung out with Jesus, God, John the Baptist, Gabriel, the Holy Ghost (I've always wondered about the holy ghost), his great grandpa, his dead sister, and Jesus's rainbow horse, and the ubiquitous men in white beards on giant thrones.<br /><br />I do not doubt for a second that this child experienced something, be it a near death experience or an illness spawned delusion, but I take issue with how his story comes about. I have an open mind about this kind of thing, but.....awe c'mon.<br /><br />So Colton gets really really sick, vomiting every 30 minutes. His parents eventually get him to a hospital where tests are administered. Even though the Burpos question whether it is appendicitis (they have a strong suspicion it is) the incompetent doctor informs them that all the tests are negative for appendicitis. Instead of ripping the child out of bed and heading to a different hospital immediately, they wait 3 or 5 days (can't remember which). By the time they get it in gear and get him to a new doctor the boy is very close to death. His appendix has ruptured and the poison circulated through him. He was dehydrated, and of course he hadn't eaten in days. Ripe for delusion I say.<br /><br />He was taken into emergency surgery, during which he has his NDE/out of body experience. Some compelling things do happen that a lines itself more with an out of body experience, he knows what his parents are doing in other rooms for instance. When Colton is in "Heaven" He sees dead relatives he had never met before. This is all I can buy.<br /><br />Whenever someone reports a NDE, they are usually consistent with that persons belief system. If you are a Christian your experience will follow what you were taught from the bible. If you were raised to believe (or not believe) in another manner, your experience will follow what you were taught. Did I mention Colton's father, the author Todd Burpo, is an evangelical preacher?<br /><br />Slowly Colton's story comes out, by means of a myriad of questions by his father (leading ones no doubt). Colton would mention stuff like what color cloths Jesus wore (purple if you wondered) and his dad's jaw would drop (this happened often) because no one had ever told Colton that Jesus wore purple. "How could he know this?" his dad wondered. I'll tell you how Todd, he heard you say it! You're a preacher, I'd gather the conversation is pretty bible heavy in your household. <br /><br />My question is how could he not know it? Three year old brains are super sponges...he heard this mentioned a some point and that's how he knew it Todd, no great mystery.<br /><br />As I was listening to this book I kept trying to keep an open mind, but where this book completely jumped the shark for me was during a part where Colton is with his dad and family at a funeral. Colton points at the casket with a stern and concerned look on his face and says "Did that man have Jesus in his heart daddy? He had to have Jesus in his heart or he won't go to Heaven!!" *insert drama sting here* and Todd's jaw dropped once again. During this I'm standing in Aldi looking at the cauliflower and said out loud AWE C'MON!! I got a few stares, so I dropped the cauliflower and said "spots".<br /><br />This book is written by a guy who really believes in his evangelicalism. He rummaged around in Colton's "memories" and got "answers" that he wanted to hear and then believes proves his evangelical Christianity. If you don't believe what he believes folks 'ya aint gettin' into heaven people...sorry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16510061730134759276" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_222148168" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/222148168" />
    <a title="Aaron Carlberg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3942519-aaron-carlberg"><img alt="Aaron Carlberg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277848015p2/3942519.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/222148168?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aaron Carlberg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aaron Carlberg" href="/user/show/3942519-aaron-carlberg">Aaron Carlberg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer222148168" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4425664503274912701">For a while now people have been asking me to read and comment on the book Heaven is for Real written by Todd Burpo. Todd Burpo writes the book as an account of the experiences of his four-year-old son, Colten. For some reason, because I am a pastor, people think I am going to love this book about a little boy who went to heaven, met Jesus, and then Jesus sent him back (like you put your unfinished cookies back in the oven).<br/><br/>Well, here goes…and don't hate me for being honest. I did not like this </span>
  <span id="freeText4425664503274912701" style="display:none">For a while now people have been asking me to read and comment on the book Heaven is for Real written by Todd Burpo. Todd Burpo writes the book as an account of the experiences of his four-year-old son, Colten. For some reason, because I am a pastor, people think I am going to love this book about a little boy who went to heaven, met Jesus, and then Jesus sent him back (like you put your unfinished cookies back in the oven).<br /><br />Well, here goes…and don't hate me for being honest. I did not like this book what-so-ever. <br /><br />This book plays into our current climate of people going to spend (pick your amount of time) minutes/hours/seconds in (pick your place) heaven/hell/purgatory/Burger King in an effort to (pick your reason) grow your faith/make you feel good/make some money. It is not just the writing style of an adult trying to sound like a kid who sounds like an adult trying to sound like a kid, it is the poor theology that people are so willing to buy into. <br /><br />First, let me just say that Todd Burpo sounds like a Christian, I am not questioning his faith (for some reason people who have commented on this review think I question His salvation, but that is not my place). I simply want to say to so many Christians out there, please use more discernment in what you allow to shape your view of heaven and Jesus. We should be very careful when we start to build theology based solely on our experience (especially that of a 4 year old). As an example, scripture teaches that Jesus won our victory on the cross, he said our sins were paid for, Jesus actually used the words, "it is finished" in John 19:30. In Heaven is for Real we are told that Jesus still has to fight (pg136-139). My question is why? If Jesus is all-powerful what is the point of him handing out swords and bows and arrows? <br /><br />It seems much of the book is written to fit into a particular mindset and belief structure. Colten (the boy) will say something and the dad will interpret it with a verse that seems to make it all make sense…but it doesn't! When trying to describe Jesus, Colten is convinced that his style of dress is still from the 1st century and has never changed. He has brown hair, a beard, a white robe with a purple sash like a beauty pagent contestant(Jesus is the only one who gets to wear purple by the way), a crown, and red marks in the center of his palms (even though that is not where the nails would have gone) and on his feet (page 65-66.)<br /><br />This goes on and on…from people getting wings, to God and Jesus apparently sitting in their big chairs all day (except when Jesus is going up and down like an elevator)...oh, I almost forgot; all Angels also have to carry swords to keep Satan out of heaven (Page 133). <br /><br />I do not just want to blast the book, but this is the problem with what Christianity has become…according to the book the reason Jesus died on the cross was so that we could go to heaven.<br /><br />Question, is that why Jesus died? <br /><br />Modern Christianity makes salvation all about us, when salvation is about Jesus. Jesus died because we were so awful, our sin so great, that we destroyed everything. Jesus' death pays for our sin not so that we could go to heaven but so we could be a redeemed people living redeemed lives in Jesus' name. Jesus rose from the dead conquering our enemies of Satan, sin, and death. Jesus' death and resurrection are about life, bringing heaven to earth in how we, as God's people, live this life.<br /><br />Never once did Jesus, in the scriptures, talk about the goal being to get out of here and go somewhere else. The purpose of His coming was to restore relationship between God and us again by removing what stood in the way, namely our sin. <br /><br />As Christians we must get away from the idea that Jesus died so we could get out of this place and watch it burn. Jesus died AND ROSE; the heart that Jesus had for this world should burn in us with a passion and intensity to see the lost know Him.<br /><br />Heaven IS for real, but heaven is NOT the point. The point is Jesus…that is what we should remember.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4425664503274912701" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2342968929" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2342968929" />
    <a title="Miranda Reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads"><img alt="Miranda Reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592243041p2/71848701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2342968929?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miranda Reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miranda Reads" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads">Miranda Reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=2018-popsugar-reading-challenge">2018-popsugar-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=itty-bitty-bois">itty-bitty-bois</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2342968929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14218812625995780845">As a true memoir <b>- astounding. </b><br/><br/>As a novel - <b>an okay read. </b><br/><br/><u>As an audiobook -<b> kill it with fire</b></u><br/><br/>There's two ways to go about this review. Either 1) discuss the <b>theological impact</b> and connotations or 2) discuss the <b>quality of the novel</b>.<br/><br/>I will be going about route 2 - therefore I won't be getting into the whole did-it-really-happen sort of or how-this-affected-my-personal-beliefs. (Note: As a rule, I don't like it when such events are <b>capitalized to this extent </b>(books, movies, merch, etc) - it just r</span>
  <span id="freeText14218812625995780845" style="display:none">As a true memoir <b>- astounding. </b><br /><br />As a novel - <b>an okay read. </b><br /><br /><u>As an audiobook -<b> kill it with fire</b></u><br /><br />There's two ways to go about this review. Either 1) discuss the <b>theological impact</b> and connotations or 2) discuss the <b>quality of the novel</b>.<br /><br />I will be going about route 2 - therefore I won't be getting into the whole did-it-really-happen sort of or how-this-affected-my-personal-beliefs. (Note: As a rule, I don't like it when such events are <b>capitalized to this extent </b>(books, movies, merch, etc) - it just rubs me the wrong way.)<br /><br />Therefore, I am <b>
  <u>
    <i>judging the book solely as I would a story.</i>
  </u>
</b> Please keep that in mind when reading my critiques.<br /><br />Most notably, the plot was just a pile of <i>meh</i>.<b> It wasn't terrible,</b> but it also didn't stand out. <br /><br />I read this book a few years ago but remembered next to nothing. I have a feeling in another few months,<b> I will forget it again.</b><br /><br />Three-year-old <b>Colton Burpo undergoes surgery</b> for a 5-day-old ruptured appendix. Months after a rather risky surgery, Colton starts saying some <b>very unique things</b> (ranging from rainbow ponies to crying about a miscarried sister he never met).<br /><br /><b>To summarize the entire book: </b> Colton says something amazing, his father marvels, his father finds references in the bible, marvels again and they move on. <b>That was literally it.</b><br /><br />His father (a preacher) maintains that most of Colton's <b> revelations came to the family unprovoked</b> and over the course of several years.<br /><br /> The fact that most of these moments come months, if not years, after the initial surgery, really <b>makes me wonder how much the kid actually remembered vs how much he thinks he remembered years later.</b><br /><br /><b>When you're a kid, your memory is fluid.</b> You can't remember if you don't like broccoli but your parents say you did last time so you try eating it again (and are disappointed again). <br /><br /> You can't tell the <b> imaginary from the real world </b> until you are around 5(ish) (according to a cursory google). Yet, plot hinges on the kid remembering a long, complicated series of events from when he was three. <b>Frustratingly convenient.</b><br /><br />His father also firmly maintained that most of the things Colton said are things that he had no way of knowing.<br /><br />Judging this as a novel...I have to say that most of these crazy-amazing-things Colton said seemed like <b>they could've been overheard</b> - if not from his parents then from others in the parish. <br /><br />Is it really so unexpected that some well-meaning parishioner didn't describe what heaven was like to the pastor's son? Or that Colton may have overheard two old ladies gossiping about his mother's miscarriage?<b> Kids are perceptive - they listen more than you think. </b><br /><br />And while his father constantly maintains that the kid could not have known x, y, and z religious concepts. I kept wanting to say, <i>really? The Pastor's son isn't going to parrot back more than the average kid about religion?</i><br /><br />Then there was the <b>
  <u> sheer amount of precociousness stuffed into the child. </u>
</b> Young Colton was like all six of Anne (Of Green Gables)'s children rolled into one.<br /><br />Everything the child did was adorably religious - from<b> feeling bad about fighting </b>(Jesus wouldn't like that) to <b>his scream-cry fit at a funeral </b>(Colton needed to know whether the dead man had Jesus in his heart). <br /><br />I just couldn't get into the style of writing and the way the characters were portrayed. All I can say is thank goodness they had the based-on-a-true-story angle otherwise this book would've tanked. <br /><br /><i> The 2018 PopSugar Reading Challenge - a best seller from the year you graduated high school</i><br /><br /><b>Audiobook Comments</b><br /><br /><u>DO NOT LISTEN TO THE AUDIOBOOK.</u><br /><br />I cannot emphasize this enough. I have listened to many, many audiobooks but this one grated my nerves the most. I knocked off an extra star because listening to it <b>ruined the experience.</b><br /><br />The narrator (Dean Gallagher) did an okay job narrating from the dad's perspective - his voice had decent tone and inflection.<br /><br />All hell broke loose when the narrator attempted Colton's voice. Every time 3-year-old Colton "spoke," I cringed.<b> Like physically cringed. </b><br /><br />There is something so, so wrong about a <b> grown man was mimicking the voice of a three-year-old</b>. <br /><br />His voice went high, breathy and had the grating-on-your nerves whine to it - especially when he said Mommy and Daddy. <br /><br />And Colton says Mommy and Daddy a lot. <br /><br /><b>Like <i> a lot,</i> a lot. </b><br /><br />To the point where every time the narrator wailed "Mommmmeeeee" I wanted to turn off the audiobook. <br /><br />After all the hundreds of thousands this family made from this book (and related paraphernalia), they seriously couldn't spring for a child actor? Or someone other than a old man to represent their child? <b> It was maddening</b><br /><br /><a href="http://bit.ly/33ii2CT" rel="nofollow noopener">YouTube</a> | <a href="http://www.mirandareads.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/miranda.reads/?hl=en" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/Miranda_Reads" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | Snapchat @miranda.reads <br /><br />Happy Reading!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14218812625995780845" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_160503477" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160503477" />
    <a title="Natasha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2162458-natasha"><img alt="Natasha" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/160503477?book_show_action=true">Apr 12, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natasha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natasha" href="/user/show/2162458-natasha">Natasha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer160503477" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6096329351163275357">I am already on the 11th chapter -<br/> <br/>I am just get chills when I read this book. Last night, I spent time reading comments from Goodreads. I was shocked of all the one stars and negative comments. These are the topics that kept coming up. I just have to vent about it...<br/><br/>1.)<b>WHY DID THE PARENTS WAIT SO LONG?</b> - That the parents waited to long to take him to the doctor.Well, they did take him to the doctor. The doctor told them no thing was wrong expect a stomach virus. This can reoccur plus when the s</span>
  <span id="freeText6096329351163275357" style="display:none">I am already on the 11th chapter -<br /> <br />I am just get chills when I read this book. Last night, I spent time reading comments from Goodreads. I was shocked of all the one stars and negative comments. These are the topics that kept coming up. I just have to vent about it...<br /><br />1.)<b>WHY DID THE PARENTS WAIT SO LONG?</b> - That the parents waited to long to take him to the doctor.Well, they did take him to the doctor. The doctor told them no thing was wrong expect a stomach virus. This can reoccur plus when the sister got sick it just made sense.<br /><br /> - Being a parent - I put ( or did ) trust into the doctors. I know how it is to feel helpless and not knowing what is 100% correct with a sick kid. I feel they did what they thought was right. It can be confusing when it comes to sick kids. <br /><br />2.)<b>THE BOY GREW UP WITH A DAD AS PASTOR</b> - The boy knew what to say because his dad is a Pastor and he grew up with all the imagery and around Christians. The way it comes across to me is .. HE IS 3 YRS OLD at the time this happen. It was so branded in his mind - he still remembers till this day! and How can they explain how he saw his dad crying in the room and mom praying on the phone? <br /><br />I am sorry - if my 3/4 year old son told me this in such conviction - I would believe him 100%. I feel this boy is innocent - he did not make up stories just because he knows who Jesus is and Heaven. He went into detail about what Jesus was wearing and etc. in so much detail - that the dad could actually pull out Bible Scriptures relating to it.<br /><br />3.) <b>PROPAGANDA</b> Comments were left also about how the book was too preachy and how he makes it come across that the only way to Heaven is through knowing Jesus. Well- my opinion - there is nothing wrong with TOO PREACHY. This book was written, I believe, as a witness tool. This may have happened to this boy, so his dad could write a book. God wants everyone saved - I think the Pastors job is DONE if only ONE person gets saved. JUST ONE! <br /><br />As for the only way to Heaven - I am not going to get into debate about theology. But I will say this - this book goes by the Bible - and YES, the only way to Heaven is through the relationship of Jesus. Period. <br /><br />4. <b> MAKE BELIEVE </b> I see some people just slamming this book without reading it. These "people" are leaving remarks about how we shouldn't believe there is a man sitting on the clouds. I know some people just like to spread their opinion but don't slam it just because it is about Heaven or Jesus or God. <br /><br />I am continuing to read this book - I should finish by the end of the week. I am so full of joy and I can't wait to go to HEAVEN. <br /><br />---------<br /><u>UPDATE</u><br />I just finished this book late last night -- All I can say is I can't wait to go to Heaven to meet Jesus.</span>

<div id="review_196252101" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/196252101" />
    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1240205-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461167626p2/1240205.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/196252101?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/1240205-amanda">Amanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer196252101" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer854030897556359739">So I'll preface this by saying the following:<br/>1.) I would have never read this of my own accord. I read it only for book club.<br/>2.) I was skeptical going in.<br/>3.) Though I'm a born-and-raised Catholic, I would classify myself as an agnostic, at best.<br/>4.) I'll also admit I skimmed huge chunks of this book. It was the only way I was going to get through it at all. It was worse than I expected. <br/><br/>Things that Bothered Me (in no particular order):<br/>1.) The first half of the book is the father rambling about hi</span>
  <span id="freeText854030897556359739" style="display:none">So I'll preface this by saying the following:<br />1.) I would have never read this of my own accord. I read it only for book club.<br />2.) I was skeptical going in.<br />3.) Though I'm a born-and-raised Catholic, I would classify myself as an agnostic, at best.<br />4.) I'll also admit I skimmed huge chunks of this book. It was the only way I was going to get through it at all. It was worse than I expected. <br /><br />Things that Bothered Me (in no particular order):<br />1.) The first half of the book is the father rambling about his illnesses. Yes, I'm very sad the guy had kidney stones and breast cancer and [some other ailment I can't remember]...but I don't see what any of that has to do with the kid getting sick. <br /><br />2.) If this was all so moving, why did they wait seven years before writing the book?<br /><br />3.) The kid is 11 now. Don't you think that's old enough that he could have wrote the book on his own? Or at least contributed a chapter in his own words?<br /><br />4.) Others have said this, but it bears repeating: isn't it convenient that this miracle happened to a pastor's kid? The dad keeps going on and on about how Colton just "couldn't have known" about so much of this religious stuff. Really? Kids are remarkably perceptive. I would find this all much more moving if it had happened to a kid who had never heard "the good word."<br /><br />5.) The parents only dragged the story out of the kid over the course of <i>years</i>. As a parent, this is extremely odd to me. If my kid started telling me one day he'd been to heaven, you better believe I'd be asking some questions, and right now, not five years later.<br /><br />Finally (and this verges on a rant), it kind of annoys me how many Christians are saying that this changed their life / moved them to be better parents / etc... I'm sorry...you have the <i>Bible</i>. The WORD OF GOD isn't enough for you? It takes a fairy tale as told by a three year old to convince you that maybe you should get your act together and start acting like a Christian?<br /><br />Fundamentally, I was just the wrong person to read this book. I am not its target audience. A belief in heaven presupposes a belief in God. Until you can sell me on that, there's not much this (poorly written, highly questionable) book is going to do for me.<br /><br />ETA (2/2012): I think it's pretty clear I think this particular book is crap. However, if you're interested in first-hand, post-death experiences, I'd suggest reading, "90 Minutes in Heaven". It's similar in its premise--a man dies, spends 90 minutes in heaven, comes back and tells his story--, BUT it's experienced by an adult and told by that same adult. (Unlike "Heaven", which is merely the dad's retelling of the kid's story.) I read it several years ago, but remember being much more impressed by it. I will offer the caveat that I was a much better Catholic/Christian at that time than I am now, so that likely colored my perception of the book. However, even accounting for that, "90 Minutes" is certainly the better written book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="854030897556359739" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_179844928" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/179844928" />
    <a title="Tina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/108010-tina"><img alt="Tina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1180903003p2/108010.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/179844928?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tina" href="/user/show/108010-tina">Tina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/108010-tina?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/108010-tina?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer179844928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2003360222273148870">First of all, this is a book I would never in a million years pick up on my own. I read it b/c one of my students brought it to me and told me how much he loved it, and that he wanted to lend it to me so I could read it. So, yeah: I read this b/c I care about my students and the things that are important to them. But I am also an atheist, and so of course I couldn't read the book through any other lens than that.<br/><br/>I honestly tried to balance being skeptical with having an open mind, but, as so man</span>
  <span id="freeText2003360222273148870" style="display:none">First of all, this is a book I would never in a million years pick up on my own. I read it b/c one of my students brought it to me and told me how much he loved it, and that he wanted to lend it to me so I could read it. So, yeah: I read this b/c I care about my students and the things that are important to them. But I am also an atheist, and so of course I couldn't read the book through any other lens than that.<br /><br />I honestly tried to balance being skeptical with having an open mind, but, as so many others have pointed out, there's just too many big flaws, such as: He's the son of a preacher and so OF COURSE would/could know all this stuff that his father seems baffled by him knowing, young kids are imaginative and he doubtless started to learn that he got lots of positive attention whenever he'd talk about Heaven, the memories were revealed over several years and I find it hard to believe that the parents asked NO leading questions (and even if they somehow managed not to, we're back to issue one and the fact that he is growing up in a religious environment and is not completely ignorant of scripture), etc. See the various other one and two star reviews for a more comprehensive discussion.<br /><br />More than anything, this book disturbed me. I'm not trying to be dramatic or sarcastic here, but the levels of indoctrination going on are unsettling. When a four year old kid starts freaking out at funerals about people who didn't "know" Jesus not going to heaven...that's alarming to me. And I grew up Catholic, so it's not like Christianity is scary b/c it's foreign. I guess what's scary is how closed off some religious people are to other religions and myths. I don't know.<br /><br />To summarize: I don't believe this and I didn't like it (the casual sexism sprinkled in didn't help, you know), but I'm certainly glad the kid didn't die. (The incompetent doctor is another scary thing, too.)<br /><br />EDITED TO ADD: Annnnnd a book on psychology that I'm currently reading just reminded me that when it comes to memory, kids under the age of 5 often can't distinguish things that actually happened to them vs. things they were told. Not that I think my skepticism needed even more justification, but, there it is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2003360222273148870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_291447060" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/291447060" />
    <a title="Dawn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2517340-dawn"><img alt="Dawn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480301169p2/2517340.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/291447060?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dawn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dawn" href="/user/show/2517340-dawn">Dawn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2517340-dawn?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2517340-dawn?shelf=other">other</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer291447060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8275831042224566415">I'm not sure how I feel about this book. When I lost my son last year, I got a lot of the "he's in heaven now, and you'll be with him again some day" comments. And I wanted to believe, I really did.. But I've always had a problem with faith. It would be so much easier for me to accept losing him, if I could truly have faith in him being safe and happy in a better place. But it's a hard thing to believe... Try though I might.<br/><br/>Several people in a Compassionate Friends support meeting recommended th</span>
  <span id="freeText8275831042224566415" style="display:none">I'm not sure how I feel about this book. When I lost my son last year, I got a lot of the "he's in heaven now, and you'll be with him again some day" comments. And I wanted to believe, I really did.. But I've always had a problem with faith. It would be so much easier for me to accept losing him, if I could truly have faith in him being safe and happy in a better place. But it's a hard thing to believe... Try though I might.<br /><br />Several people in a Compassionate Friends support meeting recommended this book to me, and said it might help me find some faith.. Might make it a little easier for me really believe that my son is in a better place. So I read it.. And I'm not sure what I feel.<br /><br />First of all, I'm not quite sure that this is a book that a parent who lost a young child should read. It tells the story of a family that almost loses a young child, and that alone triggered a lot of emotions. Longing for my son.. Sadness at his loss.. And jealousy. Because this family <i>almost</i> lost a son, while I did lose my son. <br /><br />There's a line near the end, that really upset me...<br /><br /><i>Speaking of Colton’s experience in heaven, people have said to us, “Your family is so blessed!” In the sense that we’ve had a glimpse through the veil that separates earth from eternity, they’re right. But I also think, Blessed? We watched our son almost die.</i><br /><br />I read this and I think... <b>Yes</b>, you are blessed. You watched your son <i>almost</i> die. But you have him with you now, he's growing up and is healthy and happy. You are blessed beyond belief.<br /><br />While it did give me a lot to ponder.. It also did wonders for the guilt I feel because of my loss and the circumstances surrounding it. And I mean that in a not so good way.. It goes on and on about how they prayed and prayed, and God granted them their wish, God let them keep their son. I didn't pray, because I had no faith, I didn't believe, or at the least I didn't care. I saw no need to, I didn't know he could be taken from me. Is my son gone because of my lack of prayer? Because of my lack of belief? Because I didn't appreciate his mortality? If I had prayed the right prayer, could I have saved him? Was I not a good enough person to save him? I know these questions are silly in a way. Scratch that, I know they are silly in every way. But reading this story brought the questions repeatedly into my mind, it wasn't something I could help. <br /><br />And the questions, of course, don't stop there.. Beyond those questions, were the questions that those questions inevitably led to. If the answer to those questions is yes... And it's my fault because I lacked faith.. The question isn't "Why me?" it's "Why my son?" Why did he have to be punished for my wrongs and for my lack of faith? But like I said... These sort of thoughts aren't new to me. These are questions I deal with every day, and eventually hope to work through. Reading this story just amplified them for a short period of time.<br /><br />For that reason, I definitely wouldn't recommend this book to anyone who has lost a young child and is still dealing with the grief and guilt associated with that loss. Beyond that.. I need time to think about this story, to process it, discuss it, research it. I want to believe.. I want to make the right choices so I can be with my son again one day, if that's a possibility. But to make the right choices, and to believe.. I have to do it right. I have to find real faith, not conditional faith (faith based on the condition that I'll get to be with my son). It's not something that can happen over night.. But I hope one day to find peace. <br /><br />With that, I'll end this review. It's been a revealing one, and I'm hesitant to post it. But... It's a part of who I am now. I have to embrace it or hide it.. And I won't hide Lucas.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8275831042224566415" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_944586069" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/944586069" />
    <a title="Deborah Markus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus"><img alt="Deborah Markus" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/944586069?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Markus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Markus" href="/user/show/954028-deborah-markus">Deborah Markus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer944586069" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12752108621023453589">True story #1: My husband’s former boss once told him she has evidence for a past life: she spoke Welsh (a language she is completely unfamiliar with) in her sleep.<br/><br/>My husband asked the obvious question: “How do you know you spoke Welsh in your sleep?”<br/><br/>She had a friend spending the night, and the friend heard her talking in her sleep, “and she said I was speaking Welsh.”<br/><br/>“Does <i>she</i> speak Welsh?”<br/><br/>“Well, no. But she said it <i>sounded</i> like Welsh.”<br/><br/>Every time I hear someone say, “This is how I know such-and</span>
  <span id="freeText12752108621023453589" style="display:none">True story #1: My husband’s former boss once told him she has evidence for a past life: she spoke Welsh (a language she is completely unfamiliar with) in her sleep.<br /><br />My husband asked the obvious question: “How do you know you spoke Welsh in your sleep?”<br /><br />She had a friend spending the night, and the friend heard her talking in her sleep, “and she said I was speaking Welsh.”<br /><br />“Does <i>she</i> speak Welsh?”<br /><br />“Well, no. But she said it <i>sounded</i> like Welsh.”<br /><br />Every time I hear someone say, “This is how I know such-and-such is true,” I think about this story. <br /><br />I think about the people who wouldn’t bother to ask any questions after hearing this woman announce that she spoke Welsh in her sleep. <br /><br />I think about the fact that my husband’s questions didn’t change her mind one bit about what she believes.<br /><br />I think about the fact that she’s deciding how the universe works based on one incredibly flimsy anecdote. <br /><br />I think about how she might react if I suggested that what had really happened was, the government has been messing around with telepathic language education programs, and her head accidentally got in the path of one of their experiments.<br /><br />She’d tell me I was being ridiculous, I’m sure. <br /><br />Why?<br /><br />Why is that idea any odder than the concept of an invisible, intangible spirit that flits from body to body and retains memories of some of its voyages?<br /><br />“Memory” is too strong a word for something she has no recollection of, of course. But the idea that she’s a very specific sort of immortal being – one who collects rare languages along the way! – is apparently preferable to the idea that, as Charlotte says in <i>Charlotte’s Web</i>, “We’re born, we live a little while, we die.”<br /><br />There’s plenty of evidence for <i>that,</i> but nobody likes to look at it. <br /><br />True story #2: When I was in second grade, I heard someone say something about skipping a grade in school. I’d never heard of this concept, and I was curious. This was decades before the Internet, so when I got home, I asked my mother, “How do people skip grades?”<br /><br />My mother didn’t reply in a matching matter-of-fact tone, “Oh, there’s a testing process.” To which <i>I</i> would have replied, “Oh,” and said no more about it. Because I wasn’t interested in skipping a grade myself. I just wanted to know what it was, the same way I wanted to know how caterpillars turned into butterflies and why reptiles were called “cold-blooded.”<br /><br />Instead, my mother’s eyes lit up with fondness, hope, and pride. “Do you want to skip a grade?” she asked warmly.<br /><br />I was one of four kids at the time. (More would come later.) My mother was a very troubled individual, and no one had ever taught her much about parenting or patience. The <i>only</i> time I got positive attention from her was when I successfully fulfilled my designation as “the smart one.”<br /><br />I could respond (truthfully), “Not really – I was just wondering,” and watch the glow vanish from her face. Or I could nod speechlessly and feel like a fraud for (let me check my watch) the rest of my life.<br /><br />I don’t blame her much for asking, since I can see how it might sound as if I were hinting in that direction; but I can’t much blame myself either for not having the moral fiber to answer honestly. I just couldn’t stand the thought of watching her eager expression go blank and disappointed.<br /><br />So I took the tests and got promoted a grade and listened to my parents bragging about their super-smart kid and felt a mishmash of emotions I can’t begin to break down even now. <br /><br />I thought of this story all through reading <i>HifR</i>. I thought about how my mother probably thinks she was being a supportive parent when her daughter came home begging to skip a grade. <br /><br />True story #3: My father spent a lot of my childhood unemployed, and we were broke as often as not. We never went on vacation, or even on day-trips. Nevertheless, my parents were able to come up with the money to enroll us all in a three-day seminar sure to change our lives by teaching us the Silva Mind Control method. <br /><br />This was presented as a matter-of-fact miracle factory. My sisters and I were told stories of children who performed all sorts of wonders, even curing faraway strangers suffering from fatal diseases – just by using their Silva-taught mental powers! <br /><br />The children’s classes <i>we</i> took barely touched on this sort of life-saving work, though. Instead, we were encouraged to focus on something showier and easier to check: bending cutlery just by thinking about it.<br /><br />Give a classroom full of children a spoon apiece and tell them how impressed you’ll be by anyone who can bend that thing like it had melted in their hot little hands. Emphasize this point frequently. Give the kids several hours to “perform.” Don’t pressure them by watching them too closely, and (of course!) allow them to handle the spoons as much as they like. Make sure you only have one or two grownups riding herd on 30 kids. Have those grownups step outside frequently for coffee breaks. Oh, and if you see one of the kids vigorously pushing and pulling on the neck of the spoon – bending it using brute force rather then mind-powers – assure the other children that this is known in the business as “loosening it up.”<br /><br />What will you get?<br /><br />You’ll get 28 kids with silverware bent every which way, and two dejected, bewildered children clutching spoons stubbornly set in their original spoony curves. These two will wonder where they went wrong as they watch the other kids eating up praise from the proud grownups.<br /><br />You might want to keep this story in mind as you read <i>HifR</i>. (P.S. Yes, I was one of the two. My older sister was the other. After spending several years of childhood feeling like a loser, I’m very proud of both of us.)<br /><br />True story #4: Carol Tavris relates a startling story from her own life in <i>Mistakes Were Made (but not by me): Why We Justify Foolish Beliefs, Bad Decisions, and Hurtful Acts</i>, the terrific book she co-authored with Elliot Aronson. Tavris’ favorite children’s book was James Thurber’s <i>The Wonderful O</i>, which she remembers her father giving her as a child:<br /><br />“A band of pirates takes over an island and forbids the locals to speak any word or use any object containing the letter O. I have a vivid memory of my father reading <i>The Wonderful O</i> and our laughing together at the thought of shy Ophelia Oliver saying her name without its O’s. I remember trying valiantly, along with the invaded islanders, to guess the fourth O word that must never be lost (after love, hope, and valor), and my father’s teasing guesses: Oregon? Orangutan? Ophthalmologist?”<br /><br />Isn’t that sweet? Many of us cherish similar fond memories from childhood.<br /><br />Oh, wait. I forgot the important part:<br /><br />“And then, not long ago, I found my first edition of <i>The Wonderful O</i>. It had been published in 1957, one year after my father’s death.”<br /><br />Yup.<br /><br />“I stared at that date in disbelief and shock. Obviously, someone else gave me that book, someone else read it to me, someone else laughed with me about Phelia Liver, someone else wanted me to understand that the fourth O was freedom. Someone lost to my recollection.”<br /><br />Many of us have read the many books, articles, and blog posts available on how human memory really works, and how reliable it isn’t. I’d guess that most of us shake our heads and murmur to ourselves, “Wow. It’s amazing how flawed everybody else’s memory is.”<br /><br />Stories like Carol Tavris’ make it a little harder to hold on to that comfortable sense of superiority. <br /><br />True Story #5: My teenage son is homeschooled, so he only knows things I taught him.<br /><br />Just kidding. He knows more about how my car works than I do, though I’m the one who knows how to drive it. He knows about TV shows I’ve never watched or even heard of, and we never have the TV on unless we’re all watching a movie together. He has taught himself all manner of things about electronic music production. And don’t get me started on Lego engineering. (Yes, that’s a thing.)<br /><br />Stick with me. We’re almost there.<br /><br />True Story #6, and I swear this is the last one plus it’s short: When I was six or seven, my lung collapsed. I’d always suffered from asthma, but this was worse than anything I’d ever experienced. All morning I’d been wheezing around the house, desperately hoping for some sort of improvement. Finally, I told my mother something along the lines of, “This is bad. This is different.” She rushed me to the hospital. On this short drive, a big part of my mind was occupied with the possibility that this could be The End for our young heroine. The rest was wondering how grownups always knew how to get to important places like hospitals. (Several decades later, as I consistently get lost even with the help of GPS and online map services, I’m still impressed by the driving feats my parents’ generation managed using, apparently, nothing but a compass and the stars.)<br /><br />Lots of true stories (in no particular order). Here’s where they come in.<br /><br /><i>Heaven is for Real</i> is a book whose author, Todd Burpo, spends what feels like half the story saying, “There’s no <i>way</i> my son could have known that.” Usually, “that” is something religious – specifically Protestant Christian. <br /><br />Colton, the child in question, is the son of a very engaged and active pastor. He lives in a town Burpo describes as having “more churches than banks.” Colton goes to Sunday school, and is regularly read Bible storybooks at home. But somehow, there’s “no way” he could have known that angels have halos. He absolutely, positively never heard about halos in church or Sunday school, and there’s no way he ever saw anything about halos in a picture book. See page 74 of the paperback edition if you think I’m exaggerating how vigorously the author denies the possibility that his kid could know anything about what most of us consider to be one of the two defining characteristics of angels (the other being wings, of course).<br /><br />Nearly four-year-old Colton spends five days in the hospital with an undiagnosed ruptured appendix. He’s violently ill pretty much nonstop.<br /><br />After the doctors finally figure out what the heck is wrong and remove what’s left of his appendix, Colton looks his Dad in the eye and says, “Daddy, you know I almost died.”<br /><br />Well, duh.<br /><br />I mean, no <i>way</i>:<br /><br />“Fear gripped me. <i>Where did he hear that?</i> Had he overheard the medical staff talking? Had he heard something the surgical team said, despite the anesthesia? Because we certainly hadn’t said anything about his being close to death. Sonja and I had feared he was at the brink, had <i>known</i> it after we learned his appendix had been leaking poison into his system for five days. But we’d been very careful not to say anything in front of Colton that would scare him.”<br /><br />Which I believe. What I can’t believe is that any parent is thick enough to think his dangerously afflicted kid won’t figure out that maybe, just maybe, he’s a goner. That was the first place I went in my head when my lung collapsed. I wasn’t much older than Colton, I wasn’t nearly as ill, and nobody had mentioned death or dying around me, either. Funnily enough, I figured it out from landing in the hospital after I could barely get enough air to tell my mom I needed help.<br /><br />Remember the story about the woman who “spoke Welsh” in her sleep? Burpo has a similar story about grabbing the phone and calling his wife with the news that “[Colton] told me he met John the Baptist!”<br /><br />Well, yeah. Technically. After a lot of questions from his dad, including his dad telling Colton John the Baptist’s name. <br /><br />Burpo insists all through the book that Colton offered information about Heaven all on his own – and, okay, sometimes after lots of questions from his curious parents. <br /><br />I can’t tell you how many times I stopped reading and thought (or said aloud, if my husband was there), “Objection, your honor. Counsel is leading the witness.”<br /><br />Quite aside from that: Given how much time went by between Colton’s visit to Heaven and Todd Burpo deciding to write a book about it, I can’t have much confidence in the conversations he relates from memory. Yes, those memories would be very vivid, since they relate to his faith and his son, two things Burpo cares deeply about. Carol Tavris has vivid memories about her much-loved father. Those memories turned out to be <i>how</i> trustworthy, exactly?<br /><br />Burpo insists that Colton described things on Heaven and Earth that there’s just no way he could have known about. Burpo doesn’t seem to mind, in one recorded instance, when Colton gets things wrong:<br /><br />“You said you went to heaven. People have to die to go to heaven.”<br /><br />“Well, okay then, I died. But just for a little bit.”<br /><br />Except that Burpo has the postop report from Colton’s surgery. Colton never stopped breathing. His heart never stopped beating. He <i>didn’t</i> die on the operating table.<br /><br />After a startled moment, Burpo remembers sections of the Bible “about people who had seen heaven <i>without</i> dying.” Which is fine, so far as it goes on the theological front. But Burpo doesn’t seem to notice, amidst all the “There’s no <i>way</i> he could have known that!” anecdotes, that Colton was nudged by his father’s questions into saying something <i>completely factually incorrect</i>. <br /><br />And Colton is getting plenty of incentive to keep telling stories. I’m not saying he was lying. I’m saying kids go where we push them. Burpo was calling people on the phone and inviting them over to hear the things his kid had to say about Heaven. Later, his own church was packed with people who came to hear the story he’d promised to tell about this amazing child. (And the actual kid was there for the sermon! Bonus!)<br /><br />I’ve had plenty of experience with what happens to kids who want to live up to grownup expectations. My mother would swear to this day I asked her to let me skip second grade. The other kids in the Silva class performed “magic” after being told that applying any kind of pressure they wanted to their spoons was fine – as long as the result was something that would look cool in a photo for their newsletter. (And I’m willing to bet those kids “remember” using nothing more than the force of their mental powers on that hapless cutlery.)<br /><br />To conclude (finally): I do not recommend this book unless you already agree with its premise. If you’re a critical thinker, it will drive you nuts. Also, it’s not particularly well written. (At one point, Burpo describes the kids in <i>The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe</i> as having been “deported from London” when they paid their visit to the house with the magic door. I do not think that word means what you think it means.) It’s very short, and I still had a hard time finishing it. <br /><br />If you liked this book and it made you happy: good! The world needs more happy. I’m not going to throw trash all over <i>your</i> review. Please don’t trash mine. Thank you.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12752108621023453589" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/274158895" />
    <a title="Jakob J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6853965-jakob-j"><img alt="Jakob J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586872752p2/6853965.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/274158895?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jakob J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jakob J." href="/user/show/6853965-jakob-j">Jakob J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6853965-jakob-j?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6853965-jakob-j?shelf=chloroform-in-print">chloroform-in-print</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6853965-jakob-j?shelf=purged">purged</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6853965-jakob-j?shelf=bunk">bunk</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6853965-jakob-j?shelf=pub-2010s">pub-2010s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6853965-jakob-j?shelf=work-break-reads">work-break-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6853965-jakob-j?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer274158895" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12159698107196448290"><b>Update 4/14/2014</b> (initial review remains unchanged)<br/>Now seems like as good a time as any for an update on this relevance-clutching intelligence insulter, seeing as how the <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=N-ewaCVARtM' rel='nofollow noopener'>cinematic adaptation</a> (with all the cliches and sentimentality of a <i>Lifetime</i> movie and all the subtlety and critical rigor of Ken Ham) is hitting theaters on that pagan holiday on which Christians celebrate a brutal execution and subsequent messianic zombification. The film--starring Greg Kinnear and (disappointingly) Thomas Hay</span>
  <span id="freeText12159698107196448290" style="display:none"><b>Update 4/14/2014</b> (initial review remains unchanged)<br>Now seems like as good a time as any for an update on this relevance-clutching intelligence insulter, seeing as how the <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=N-ewaCVARtM" rel="nofollow noopener">cinematic adaptation</a> (with all the cliches and sentimentality of a <i>Lifetime</i> movie and all the subtlety and critical rigor of Ken Ham) is hitting theaters on that pagan holiday on which Christians celebrate a brutal execution and subsequent messianic zombification. The film--starring Greg Kinnear and (disappointingly) Thomas Hayden Church--will, I suspect, take some narrative liberties as pertains to the source material and try to make it more palatable to general (i.e. non-Christian) audiences. This is evidenced by the casting, which is in turn evidence that some powerful casting agents were behind it. But, oh well, they're just faces for hire, and they'll class up the preposterous endeavor a little bit. The real reason I'm issuing this addendum (aside from shamelessly trying to accumulate a few more votes [ahem]) is that, if you read my original review, I took similar sneering jabs at two components of the inevitable commercial implications of this book. The first was that a version of the book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11351338-heaven-is-for-real-for-kids" rel="nofollow noopener">for kids</a> was published. Since then, we have had a version <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17034860-heaven-is-for-real-for-little-ones" rel="nofollow noopener">for infants</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11652624-heaven-is-for-real-conversation-guide" rel="nofollow noopener">a conversation guide</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13807532-heaven-changes-everything" rel="nofollow noopener">a devotional</a>, <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Heaven-For-Real-DVD-Based-Conversation/dp/1418550663/ref=pd_sim_b_16?ie=UTF8&amp;refRID=0TWZXPSTFPST7YDZJKH2" rel="nofollow noopener">and a DVD-based conversation kit</a>. This is all for that one kid's little story. The second point I made was about a book that rode on its coattails called <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8100288-the-boy-who-came-back-from-heaven" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Boy Who Came Back from Heaven</i>
</a>. Of course, that was only the beginning. An entire genre of exploiting children and hallucinatory speculation flooded the shelves, testing the credulity of the American public, and succeeding wildly. The publishers have made a killing on their rube-based buying demographic. Among these dime-a-dozen accounts are <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17164633-waking-up-in-heaven" rel="nofollow noopener">Waking Up in Heaven</a>, <a href="http://www.samharris.org/blog/item/this-must-be-heaven" rel="nofollow noopener">Proof of Heaven</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17836477-when-will-the-heaven-begin" rel="nofollow noopener">When Will the Heaven Begin?</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13158130-to-heaven-and-back?ac=1" rel="nofollow noopener">To Heaven and Back</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13789028-my-journey-to-heaven" rel="nofollow noopener">My Journey to Heaven</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7570892-flight-to-heaven?ac=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Flight to Heaven</a></i> (published earlier, but seized upon after the success of the example under discussion), and I predict many, many more before this nonsense fizzles out like Hal Lindsey and Billy Graham before them, heaven forbid. This anecdotal stupidity has created a marketing monster, and it's about time that it is slain. Reject this movie. Reject this book. Reject anything that strains credulity past its breaking point and demands that you abandon centuries of mounting scientific evidence regarding our world and our critical inquiry. The Burpos and their profiteers are now veritable snake-oil salespeople schilling an invisible product with a net benefit to the consumer of absolutely zero (at least in <i>this</i> life). Demand more respect for your cognitive faculties. <br><br>-------------------------------------<br>Reviewing this book from my standpoint will probably be as functionally useful as explaining to my devout, border-line senile great-aunt Edna why I abandoned the family tradition of Norwegian Lutheranism for methodological naturalism/ critical inquiry, proceeding to suggest such an outlook to her in her twilight years, and perhaps just as surreal. Even reading it seemed rather evil-twin-like of me.<br><br>If I ever mention that I read this book to some bottom-feeding bookstore lady who would gasp and go into a tizzy about how she cried and cried and now just can’t wait to go to heaven and sit in Jesus’ lap like little baby Burpo, I may go the intellectually rapacious route and destroy her just as she thought she found a kindred spirit; using such deliberately incendiary terms as <i>brain rape, wish-thinking porn,</i> and <i>credulity stroking</i>, or I may just smile, and in a most covert fashion, spit out a sardonic <i>’see you there’</i>. The scenario is all too possible, because it's selling better than most science books.<br><br>This book is for the minds of those who are capable of taking it seriously. Even going from Mitch Albom, or Rhonda Byrne to this, one might notice a spike in eye-rolling and scoffing and ‘you-have-got-to-be-kidding-me'. Several years of prodding parents and all we get from the kid is <i>‘Jesus has pretty eyes’, ‘I saw mommy and daddy praying for me at the hospital while I was being operated on’, ‘I saw grampa in heaven, but he wasn’t wearing his glasses’, ‘mommy, I had a sister but she died in your belly; she’s in heaven too’</i>, and Heaven’s lighting system is different from what we’re used to (probably not fluorescent; that’d be hellish), or something like that. That last one might actually be from Mitch Albom or Don Piper; all this middle-America, low-brow nonsense tends to coagulate into one heaping pile. I have to wonder if these people know that their books aren’t impervious to the secular world. Those who don’t forego their critical faculties <i>are</i> going to get their hands on this. I would honestly love to meet a skeptic who had a change-of-mind (or heart?) after reading the Burpos' tale. But then again, the family has made a pretty penny, so they probably don't much care. Oh, and in case you feel they need further support, or if you think you might need further inspiration (or if your kids do), you can get your hands on <a href="http://www.amazon.com/Heaven-Real-Kids-Little-Astounding/dp/140031870X" rel="nofollow noopener">this.</a><br><br><b>Face Value:</b><br>Assuming Mr. Burpo is telling the brimming, scrupulous truth to the best of his abilities (of which there is reason to doubt), those abilities seem to be lacking. He was utterly astonished at everything his son said regarding heaven. Every description corresponded rather well with the white-jumpsuit-with-wings-on-an-endless-expanse-of-giant-inflatable-fun-bounce-clouds-whilst-singing sort of cliché that we were forced to imagine in Sunday school. Or was it more like the garden of Eden, with a landscape that only the most austere of ethereal suburbanites could maintain? With the knowledge that his son Colton was never pronounced clinically dead (he wouldn't have gotten away with suggesting that), he never treats this toddler's testimony with a smidgen of skepticism or scrutiny. Isn’t Mr. Burpo familiar with the adage/ 90’s television series <i>kids say the darndest things</i>? But perhaps the Bible is right when it declares <i>"...unless you turn and become like children, you will never enter the kingdom of heaven".</i> Hey, what a great tagline for the book! (I wonder if it ever comes up in the contents...) However, I do not recall an affirmation of heaven being <i>"like a mustard seed".</i><br><br><b>Literary Quality:</b><br>N/A viz. there is none to speak of. (Are we sure the kid himself didn’t write it?)<br><br><b>Am I still talking about this?</b><br>This book was so stupid I nearly fell off my chair/veered off into a ditch/ spit out my coffee/ wrecked my segway/ whatever else Mr. Burpo claimed he nearly succumbed to each time his son said something that his young mind ‘couldn’t possibly have known’, or about what heaven was like, in attempts to convey bewilderment and profundity, which were definitely <i>not </i>mundane hyperbole… <br><br><b>But Hey:</b><br>If for some ungodly reason this didn’t convince you that heaven is for real, perhaps this one will (conveniently published not long after Mr. Burpo’s tale):<br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8100288.The_Boy_Who_Came_Back_from_Heaven_A_Remarkable_Account_of_Miracles__Angels__and_Life_beyond_This_World" title="The Boy Who Came Back from Heaven A Remarkable Account of Miracles, Angels, and Life beyond This World by Kevin Malarkey" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348447231s/8100288.jpg" alt="The Boy Who Came Back from Heaven A Remarkable Account of Miracles, Angels, and Life beyond This World by Kevin Malarkey" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>If none of this curtails your doubt; if indeed you are incredulous about a preacher's relaying of a child's ongoing testimony, well, there may just be no saving you. Perhaps we should all pray for a near-death experience ourselves.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12159698107196448290" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9866594111878117683">I know, I know, don't raise your eyebrows... I felt I just had to read this book. I felt it could not possibly be as ridiculous as it seemed from many reviews.<br/><br/>Well...<br/><br/><blockquote>“Colton, you said that angels sang to you while you were at the hospital?”<br/><br/>He nodded his head vigorously.<br/><br/>“What did they sing to you?”<br/><br/>Colton turned his eyes up and to the right, the attitude of remembering. “Well, they sang ‘Jesus Loves Me’ and ‘Joshua Fought the Battle of Jericho,’” he said earnestly. “I asked them to sing ‘We Will, </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText9866594111878117683" style="display:none">I know, I know, don't raise your eyebrows... I felt I just had to read this book. I felt it could not possibly be as ridiculous as it seemed from many reviews.<br /><br />Well...<br /><br /><blockquote>“Colton, you said that angels sang to you while you were at the hospital?”<br /><br />He nodded his head vigorously.<br /><br />“What did they sing to you?”<br /><br />Colton turned his eyes up and to the right, the attitude of remembering. “Well, they sang ‘Jesus Loves Me’ and ‘Joshua Fought the Battle of Jericho,’” he said earnestly. “I asked them to sing ‘We Will, We Will Rock You,’ but they wouldn’t sing that.”<br /><br />----------------------------------<br /><br />But all I could think to ask was: “So what did the kids look like? What do people look like in heaven?”<br /><br />“Everybody’s got wings,” Colton said.<br /><br />Wings, huh?<br /><br />“Did you have wings?” I asked.<br /><br />“Yeah, but mine weren’t very big.” He looked a little glum when he said this.<br /><br />“Okay . . . did you walk places or did you fly?”<br /><br />“We flew. Well, all except for Jesus. He was the only one in heaven who didn’t have wings. Jesus just went up and down like an elevator.”<br /><br />----------------------------------<br /><br />“Everyone kind of looks like angels in heaven, Dad.”<br /><br />“What do you mean?”<br /><br />“All the people have a light above their head.”<br />----------------------------------</blockquote><br /><br />I also learnt other things such as:<br /><br />1. The Burpos' first child, which was lost in a miscarriage, is alive in heaven. It is a little girl with dark hair. How the foetus grew up to this level and then stopped is unexplained, however. <br /><br />2. Similarly, Burpo's grandfather who died in his sixties has been brought back to his late twenties. Don't ask why: God works in mysterious ways. <br /><br />3. Jesus shoots down his power to Pastor Todd Burpo, Colton's father, when he preaches. Presumably he does this for all priests - which should keep him busy round the clock.<br /><br />4. God loves everybody - you can't believe how much. But then, how do such things as the recent Nepal earthquake happen?<br /><br />There should be many more - but at this point I stopped reading.<br /><br />***<br /><br />And oh, one more thing - Colton learned all this and more in three minutes of temporal time. But then maybe, time dilates up there. I'm sure it has got something to do with relativity.<br /> </span>

    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3127379-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1294876215p2/3127379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/3127379-carol">Carol</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>
